This is particularly useful in the case of non-destructive testing, where the goal is to assess the structural integrity of a building without causing additional damage. A study demonstrated the use of a mechanics-informed autoencoder to automate the detection and localization of unforeseen structural damage, showing that the integration of mechanical characteristics into the model improved the accuracy and speed of damage identification [20]. This approach highlights the potential of combining DOFS with advanced data analytics to enhance the efficiency and effectiveness of structural health monitoring.

In addition to their technical capabilities, distributed optical fiber sensors also offer practical advantages in terms of cost and ease of installation. Unlike traditional vibration monitoring systems that require a large number of discrete sensors and complex wiring, DOFS systems can be implemented with a single fiber, reducing the overall cost and complexity of the monitoring infrastructure. This makes them particularly suitable for large-scale applications, such as monitoring bridges, tunnels, and other critical infrastructure. A study on the use of DOFS in vibration monitoring of bridges demonstrated that the integration of optical fiber sensors with existing telecommunication infrastructure enabled the collection of high-quality vibration data without the need for dedicated sensors [30]. This approach not only reduces the installation and maintenance costs but also enhances the scalability of the monitoring system.

In conclusion, the role of distributed optical fiber sensors in vibration analysis of buildings is becoming increasingly significant. Their ability to provide real-time, high-resolution data, combined with the power of advanced signal processing and deep learning techniques, makes them a powerful tool for structural health monitoring. As research in this field continues to advance, the integration of DOFS with emerging technologies such as IoT, cloud computing, and AI is expected to further enhance their capabilities, enabling more accurate and efficient monitoring of building vibrations. This will not only improve the safety and reliability of civil infrastructure but also support the development of smarter and more resilient urban environments.

### 4.3 Strain and Temperature Monitoring in Infrastructure

Distributed optical fiber sensors (DOFS) have emerged as a revolutionary solution for continuous strain and temperature monitoring in civil infrastructure. Their ability to provide real-time, high-resolution data over extended distances makes them particularly suitable for applications where structural integrity is paramount. By leveraging the unique properties of optical fibers, such as their ability to detect minute changes in strain and temperature, DOFS offer a reliable and efficient means of monitoring the health of structures ranging from bridges and buildings to tunnels and pipelines. 

One of the primary advantages of DOFS in strain and temperature monitoring is their high spatial resolution. Unlike traditional point sensors, which provide localized measurements, DOFS can monitor an entire structure simultaneously, capturing variations in strain and temperature across a large area. This is particularly beneficial for detecting early signs of structural degradation, which often manifest as subtle changes in these parameters. For instance, in the case of bridges, DOFS can identify micro-cracks or localized deformations that might otherwise go unnoticed with conventional monitoring techniques [6].

Strain monitoring using DOFS is typically achieved through technologies such as Fiber Bragg Grating (FBG) sensors and Brillouin Optical Time-Domain Analysis (BOTDA). FBG sensors, for example, operate by reflecting a specific wavelength of light, which shifts in response to strain or temperature changes. This shift can be measured with high precision, allowing for accurate determination of the physical conditions affecting the structure. BOTDA, on the other hand, relies on the interaction between light and acoustic waves in the fiber to measure strain and temperature along the entire length of the fiber. Both methods benefit from the inherent durability and immunity to electromagnetic interference of optical fibers, making them ideal for long-term monitoring in harsh environments [10].

Temperature monitoring with DOFS is equally important, as thermal fluctuations can significantly affect the mechanical properties of materials used in construction. Optical fibers are sensitive to temperature changes, and this sensitivity can be harnessed to monitor temperature distribution across a structure. This is particularly useful in applications such as monitoring the thermal behavior of concrete in bridges or the temperature gradients in tunnels. For instance, in the context of tunnel monitoring, DOFS can detect temperature variations that may indicate subsurface water movement or thermal stress, which are critical factors in assessing the long-term stability of the structure [6].

The reliability of DOFS in detecting early signs of structural degradation is further enhanced by their ability to provide continuous, real-time data. This is a significant improvement over traditional methods, which often rely on periodic inspections that may miss critical changes occurring between inspection intervals. Real-time data allows for immediate response to potential issues, reducing the risk of catastrophic failures. For example, in the case of pipelines, continuous monitoring can detect leaks or pressure changes that might indicate structural weaknesses, enabling timely maintenance and preventing costly repairs [22].

Moreover, the integration of machine learning and advanced signal processing techniques with DOFS further enhances their capabilities in strain and temperature monitoring. These technologies enable the extraction of meaningful insights from the vast amounts of data generated by DOFS, improving the accuracy and efficiency of structural health monitoring. For example, deep learning algorithms can be trained to recognize patterns in strain and temperature data that are indicative of specific types of structural damage or degradation. This not only improves the detection of anomalies but also allows for predictive maintenance, where potential issues can be addressed before they escalate into serious problems [12].

The application of DOFS in strain and temperature monitoring is not limited to new constructions; they are also highly effective in the retrofitting of existing structures. Many aging infrastructures require continuous monitoring to ensure their safety and functionality. DOFS can be embedded within existing structures or attached to their surfaces, providing a non-intrusive and cost-effective solution for long-term monitoring. For instance, in the case of historical buildings, DOFS can be used to monitor the structural health without compromising the integrity of the original materials [13].

Another significant advantage of DOFS is their scalability. As infrastructure projects grow in complexity and scale, the ability to monitor large areas with a single fiber becomes increasingly valuable. This is particularly relevant in the context of smart cities, where the integration of DOFS with other sensor technologies and communication systems can enable comprehensive monitoring of urban infrastructure. For example, the use of DOFS in conjunction with the Internet of Things (IoT) can provide a real-time, centralized monitoring system that enhances the resilience and efficiency of urban environments [11].

In conclusion, distributed optical fiber sensors have become an indispensable tool for continuous strain and temperature monitoring in civil infrastructure. Their high spatial resolution, reliability, and real-time capabilities make them well-suited for detecting early signs of structural degradation, ensuring the safety and longevity of infrastructure. The integration of advanced technologies such as machine learning further enhances their effectiveness, enabling more accurate and efficient monitoring. As the demand for intelligent and resilient infrastructure continues to grow, the role of DOFS in structural health monitoring is expected to become even more critical. The ongoing advancements in DOFS technology and data analysis methods will undoubtedly contribute to the development of more sophisticated and reliable monitoring systems for the future.

### 4.4 Deformation Tracking in Tunnels and Underground Structures

Deformation tracking in tunnels and underground structures is a critical component of structural health monitoring (SHM) that ensures the safety, stability, and longevity of these infrastructures. Tunnels and underground structures, such as subways, underground reservoirs, and mines, are subjected to various mechanical and environmental stresses, including ground movement, seismic activity, and the weight of surrounding soil and rock. These forces can cause gradual deformations that, if undetected, may lead to catastrophic failures. Distributed optical fiber sensors (DOFS) have emerged as a promising solution for real-time deformation tracking due to their high sensitivity, spatial resolution, and ability to monitor large areas with a single fiber. This subsection explores the application of DOFS in tracking deformations in tunnels and underground structures, highlighting their capability to detect minute changes in geometry and provide early warnings of potential failures.

One of the key advantages of DOFS is their ability to measure strain and temperature along the length of the fiber, which can be used to infer deformation. Fiber Bragg Grating (FBG) sensors, a type of DOFS, are particularly effective in this regard. FBG sensors work by reflecting a specific wavelength of light, which shifts in response to strain or temperature changes. This shift can be measured with high precision, allowing for the detection of even the smallest deformations. For instance, in a study by [16], the authors demonstrated the use of FBG sensors for continuous monitoring of structural integrity in civil infrastructures, highlighting their potential for deformation tracking in tunnels. Similarly, [45] discussed the use of compressive sensing techniques to reduce the amount of data transmitted from DOFS, making it feasible to monitor large-scale underground structures efficiently.

Another important type of DOFS is Distributed Acoustic Sensing (DAS), which uses the Rayleigh backscattering effect in optical fibers to detect vibrations and acoustic signals. DAS is particularly useful for monitoring dynamic deformations and identifying sudden changes in the structural behavior of tunnels and underground structures. In a study by [15], the authors demonstrated the effectiveness of DAS in detecting structural changes through vibration analysis, a method that can be adapted for monitoring tunnels. This capability is crucial for early warning systems, as it allows for the detection of micro-cracks and other forms of damage that may not be visible to the naked eye.

Brillouin Optical Time-Domain Analysis (BOTDA) is another DOFS technology that is widely used for deformation tracking. BOTDA measures the Brillouin scattering of light in optical fibers to determine strain and temperature. This method offers high spatial resolution and is well-suited for monitoring long tunnels and underground structures. In [57], the authors proposed a framework for translating the structural condition of one civil structure to another, which can be enhanced with BOTDA for precise deformation tracking. The high sensitivity of BOTDA makes it an ideal choice for detecting the subtle deformations that can indicate the onset of structural failure.

The integration of DOFS with advanced signal processing techniques further enhances their capabilities in deformation tracking. For example, the use of deep learning algorithms, such as Convolutional Neural Networks (CNNs) and Long Short-Term Memory (LSTM) networks, can improve the accuracy of deformation detection by analyzing temporal-spatial data matrices. In [2], the authors demonstrated the effectiveness of CNNs in detecting structural degradation using time-series sensor data. This approach can be adapted for deformation tracking in tunnels and underground structures, providing real-time insights into structural behavior.

Additionally, the deployment of DOFS in tunnels and underground structures requires careful consideration of environmental and operational factors. Temperature fluctuations, humidity, and mechanical stress can affect the performance of DOFS, necessitating the development of robust calibration and maintenance procedures. [23] discussed the importance of sensor fault detection and tolerance in ensuring the reliability of SHM systems. This is particularly relevant for DOFS, as their performance can be compromised by environmental conditions and mechanical wear over time.

In practical applications, DOFS have been successfully used to monitor the deformation of tunnels and underground structures. For instance, [30] described a system that uses existing telecommunication cables for Distributed Acoustic Sensing (DAS) to collect bridge dynamic strain responses. This approach can be adapted for monitoring tunnels, where existing infrastructure can be utilized to reduce costs and improve scalability. The ability to repurpose existing fiber-optic networks for SHM is a significant advantage, as it minimizes the need for new installations and reduces the overall cost of monitoring.

Moreover, the integration of DOFS with Internet of Things (IoT) and cloud computing technologies has enabled the development of scalable and efficient SHM systems. [29] highlighted the potential of IoT in facilitating real-time data collection and analysis for SHM. By leveraging IoT platforms, DOFS can be integrated into a network of sensors that provide continuous monitoring and early warning of structural issues. This is particularly beneficial for tunnels and underground structures, where the ability to detect and respond to deformations in real-time can prevent catastrophic failures.

In summary, the application of distributed optical fiber sensors in deformation tracking for tunnels and underground structures represents a significant advancement in structural health monitoring. The high sensitivity, spatial resolution, and real-time capabilities of DOFS make them an ideal solution for monitoring the subtle deformations that can indicate the onset of structural failure. The integration of DOFS with advanced signal processing techniques and IoT technologies further enhances their effectiveness, enabling the development of scalable and efficient SHM systems. As research continues to advance, the role of DOFS in ensuring the safety and longevity of tunnels and underground structures is expected to grow, offering a promising future for structural health monitoring in civil engineering.

### 4.5 Integration with Machine Learning and Deep Learning

The integration of distributed optical fiber sensors with machine learning (ML) and deep learning (DL) algorithms has emerged as a powerful approach to enhance data interpretation, enabling robust anomaly detection, predictive maintenance, and automated damage localization in structural health monitoring (SHM). This synergy leverages the high spatial resolution and real-time capabilities of distributed optical fiber sensors with the advanced pattern recognition and decision-making capabilities of ML and DL techniques, significantly improving the accuracy and efficiency of structural monitoring systems. By combining these technologies, engineers and researchers can detect subtle structural changes, predict potential failures, and optimize maintenance strategies, ultimately leading to enhanced safety and longevity of civil infrastructure.

One of the key applications of this integration is in anomaly detection. Distributed optical fiber sensors generate vast amounts of data, which can be challenging to interpret using conventional methods. However, by employing ML and DL algorithms, such as convolutional neural networks (CNNs) and long short-term memory (LSTM) networks, researchers can extract meaningful features from the data and identify anomalies with high accuracy. For example, the integration of CNNs with distributed optical fiber sensors has been shown to effectively detect structural damage by analyzing time-series data from vibration and strain measurements [2]. These models can learn complex spatial and temporal patterns, enabling the early identification of damage that may not be visible through traditional inspection methods. Additionally, the use of LSTM networks, which are well-suited for sequential data processing, allows for the detection of long-term trends and anomalies that may indicate structural degradation over time [22].

Predictive maintenance is another critical area where the integration of distributed optical fiber sensors with ML and DL algorithms has shown significant promise. By continuously monitoring structural conditions, these systems can predict when maintenance will be required, reducing the risk of unexpected failures and minimizing downtime. For instance, the integration of machine learning techniques with distributed optical fiber sensors has been used to predict the remaining useful life of structures by analyzing historical data and identifying patterns that indicate potential failures [63]. These models can also be trained to recognize early signs of damage, enabling proactive maintenance strategies that can prevent catastrophic failures. Furthermore, the use of generative adversarial networks (GANs) has been explored to generate synthetic data for training ML models, addressing the challenge of limited real-world data in SHM applications [25].

Automated damage localization is another area where the integration of distributed optical fiber sensors with ML and DL algorithms has proven to be highly effective. Traditional methods for damage localization often rely on manual inspection and require significant expertise, which can be time-consuming and costly. By contrast, ML and DL techniques can automatically identify the location and severity of damage based on the data collected by distributed optical fiber sensors. For example, the use of deep learning algorithms has been shown to improve the accuracy of damage localization by analyzing the spatial distribution of strain and vibration data [20]. These models can learn to distinguish between normal and abnormal conditions, enabling the automatic identification of damaged areas within a structure. Additionally, the integration of attention mechanisms in DL models has been explored to enhance the localization process by focusing on the most relevant features in the data [18].

The integration of distributed optical fiber sensors with ML and DL algorithms also offers significant advantages in terms of scalability and adaptability. These technologies can be easily integrated into existing monitoring systems, enabling the deployment of large-scale SHM networks that can cover extensive infrastructure. For example, the use of distributed ML techniques has been explored to manage the data generated by multiple sensors in a network, allowing for efficient processing and analysis [64]. Additionally, the use of edge computing platforms, such as field-programmable gate arrays (FPGAs), has been investigated to enable real-time processing of data at the edge of the network, reducing latency and improving the responsiveness of SHM systems [24].

Moreover, the integration of distributed optical fiber sensors with ML and DL algorithms has opened up new possibilities for the development of intelligent SHM systems that can adapt to changing conditions and continuously improve their performance. For instance, the use of self-supervised learning techniques has been explored to train models on unlabeled data, reducing the need for extensive labeled datasets [9]. These techniques can help to improve the robustness of SHM systems by enabling them to learn from new data and adapt to different environments. Furthermore, the use of federated learning has been proposed to enable collaborative learning across multiple devices and systems, improving the accuracy and generalization of ML models while preserving data privacy [65].

In conclusion, the integration of distributed optical fiber sensors with machine learning and deep learning algorithms has significantly enhanced the capabilities of structural health monitoring systems. By leveraging the strengths of both technologies, researchers and engineers can achieve more accurate and efficient monitoring of civil infrastructure, leading to improved safety, reliability, and longevity of structures. As the field continues to evolve, the development of more advanced ML and DL techniques will further enhance the performance of SHM systems, enabling the creation of intelligent and adaptive monitoring solutions that can meet the demands of modern infrastructure.

### 4.6 Real-Time Monitoring and Early Warning Systems

Real-time monitoring and early warning systems are crucial components of structural health monitoring (SHM) in civil engineering. Distributed optical fiber sensors (DOFS) have become a pivotal technology in this domain, offering unprecedented capabilities in detecting structural anomalies, predicting potential failures, and enhancing the safety of infrastructure. These sensors enable continuous and precise monitoring, providing data that can be analyzed in real-time to identify early signs of structural degradation, such as strain, temperature fluctuations, or vibrations, which are critical for ensuring the longevity and safety of civil structures [30].

The implementation of DOFS in real-time SHM systems is characterized by their high spatial resolution, long-range monitoring capabilities, and robustness against environmental interferences. For example, Distributed Acoustic Sensing (DAS) technology, which leverages fiber optic cables to detect and locate vibrations, has been widely adopted for monitoring bridges and tunnels [30]. This technology enables the continuous capture of dynamic responses, such as those induced by traffic or seismic activity, and can trigger early warning signals if abnormal patterns are detected. The integration of machine learning algorithms further enhances the effectiveness of DOFS in real-time monitoring, as demonstrated by studies that use convolutional neural networks (CNNs) to classify vibration events and identify potential structural issues [22].

Real-time monitoring systems incorporating DOFS are particularly valuable in scenarios where immediate action is required to prevent catastrophic failures. For instance, in the context of bridges, the deployment of DOFS allows for the continuous surveillance of strain and vibration patterns, which can be used to detect subtle changes in the structural behavior that might indicate the onset of damage [30]. Such systems are essential for large-scale infrastructures, where even minor structural issues can escalate into significant problems if left unaddressed.

Moreover, the early warning systems facilitated by DOFS have the potential to significantly improve safety in civil engineering projects. These systems can provide alerts to engineers and maintenance personnel, enabling them to take proactive measures before structural failures occur. The integration of DOFS with Internet of Things (IoT) technologies and cloud computing platforms further enhances the scalability and efficiency of real-time monitoring systems, allowing for the collection, processing, and analysis of vast amounts of data from multiple sensors [29]. For example, the TelecomTM system utilizes pre-existing telecommunication fiber-optic cables as virtual sensors to monitor traffic and detect vehicle-induced vibrations, providing real-time insights into the health of road infrastructure [28].

Another critical aspect of real-time monitoring systems is the ability to process and interpret data efficiently. This is where edge computing and machine learning play a vital role. By deploying lightweight and efficient models at the edge, such as field-programmable gate arrays (FPGAs), DOFS systems can achieve low-latency processing, which is essential for real-time decision-making [24]. This approach not only reduces the burden on cloud resources but also ensures that critical information is available immediately, allowing for prompt responses to emerging threats.

Furthermore, the combination of DOFS with advanced signal processing techniques, such as compressive sensing and data reconstruction, enhances the accuracy and reliability of real-time monitoring systems. These techniques are particularly useful in scenarios where data may be sparse or corrupted, as they enable the recovery of critical information from incomplete or noisy signals [66]. For instance, the use of deep learning algorithms for signal classification and anomaly detection has been shown to significantly improve the performance of DOFS in identifying and localizing structural damage [67].

In addition to their technical advantages, real-time monitoring systems based on DOFS also offer economic benefits. By enabling the early detection of structural issues, these systems can reduce the need for costly repairs and maintenance, thereby extending the lifespan of infrastructure. The integration of DOFS with predictive maintenance strategies further enhances their value, as it allows for the optimization of maintenance schedules and resource allocation based on real-time data [29].

The role of DOFS in real-time monitoring and early warning systems is further exemplified by their application in smart cities and urban infrastructure. These systems are designed to monitor the health of various structures, including buildings, bridges, and tunnels, providing data that can be used to inform urban planning and maintenance strategies [3]. For example, the deployment of DOFS in smart cities can help identify areas of high traffic congestion, assess the structural integrity of buildings, and detect potential hazards, such as cracks or deformations, which can be addressed before they become critical.

In conclusion, the implementation of distributed optical fiber sensors in real-time structural health monitoring systems has revolutionized the way infrastructure is monitored and maintained. These sensors provide the necessary tools for detecting early signs of structural degradation, enabling proactive measures to prevent failures and improve safety. The integration of advanced technologies, such as machine learning, edge computing, and IoT, further enhances the capabilities of these systems, making them more efficient, scalable, and reliable. As the field continues to evolve, the potential of DOFS in real-time monitoring and early warning systems will only continue to grow, contributing to the development of safer and more resilient civil infrastructure [30].

### 4.7 Case Studies in Bridge and Building Monitoring

Distributed optical fiber sensors have been extensively applied in the monitoring of bridges and buildings, demonstrating their effectiveness in detecting structural damage and providing actionable data for maintenance and repair. These sensors, including Fiber Bragg Grating (FBG), Distributed Acoustic Sensing (DAS), and Brillouin Optical Time-Domain Analysis (BOTDA), have shown remarkable capabilities in real-time monitoring, high spatial resolution, and long-range sensing. Several case studies illustrate their successful implementation in various civil engineering projects.

One notable case study involves the monitoring of a steel railway bridge using FBG sensors. The study employed strain data captured from FBG sensors at 108 locations along the bridge superstructure to develop a digital twin using the statistical finite element method (statFEM). This digital twin was able to predict the "true" system response while accounting for uncertainties in sensor readings, applied loading, and finite element model errors. The system successfully reconstructed longitudinal strain distributions along the two main I-beams during the passage of a passenger train. The digital twin's ability to generate reasonable strain distribution predictions at locations without measurement data highlights the potential of distributed optical fiber sensors in optimizing sensor placement and performing reliable "what-if" analyses [31].

Another significant case study is the implementation of the TelecomTM system, which uses pre-existing telecommunication fiber-optic cables as virtual strain sensors to monitor vehicle-induced ground vibrations. This system, which operates as a software-based representation of a physical sensor, enabled fine-grained and ubiquitous traffic monitoring. The system successfully characterized the geographic location and analyzed the signal pattern of each virtual sensor through driving tests. A spatial-domain Bayesian filtering and smoothing algorithm was developed to detect, track, and characterize each vehicle. The approach leveraged the spatial dependency of multiple virtual sensors and Newton's laws of motion to combine distributed sensor data, reducing uncertainties in vehicle detection and tracking. In a real-world evaluation on a two-way traffic road with 1120 virtual sensors, TelecomTM achieved 90.18% vehicle detection accuracy and significant reductions in position and speed tracking errors [28].

In the context of bridges, the use of distributed optical fiber sensors for vibration-based health monitoring has been demonstrated in several studies. A case study involving a concrete bridge with fiber cables running in a conduit under the deck showcased the application of DAS for collecting bridge dynamic strain responses. A two-module physics-guided system identification method was developed to extract damage-sensitive information from noisy DAS data. The system successfully identified modal frequencies and reconstructed meter-scale mode shapes, proving the potential of using existing telecommunication cables for structural health monitoring without the need for dedicated sensors [30].

Another case study focused on the real-time monitoring of a highway bridge in Italy, where distributed optical fiber sensors were used for vibration analysis. The study implemented an efficient and scalable anomaly detection pipeline that did not require sending raw data to the cloud. Instead, it relied on edge computation to reduce network traffic and minimize cloud resource utilization. The system achieved a significant reduction in data communication and cloud computing costs while maintaining anomaly detection accuracy. This approach demonstrated the feasibility of using distributed optical fiber sensors for long-term structural health monitoring in real-world conditions [68].

In the case of buildings, distributed optical fiber sensors have been used to monitor vibrations and detect structural anomalies. A case study involving a steel railway bridge and a concrete girder bridge evaluated the effectiveness of a multimetric IoT sensor integrated into a cloud-based monitoring system. The system included five strain sensing axes and three acceleration axes, along with an event-driven power management system. The system was able to achieve long-term operation through power management and solar panel charging. Real-time data transmission to the server was accomplished using low-power LTE CAT.1 connection, and a displacement fusion technique was used to obtain reference-free structural displacement for ambient structural assessment [51].

Furthermore, the integration of distributed optical fiber sensors with augmented reality (AR) systems has been explored in the context of bridge monitoring. A study developed a human-centered interface that provided inspectors with real-time access to actionable structural data, such as real-time displacements under loads. The interface used a combination of Wireless Smart Sensors (WSS) and AR to allow inspectors to observe and compare displacement data, share it across space and time, and visualize displacements in time history. The system was validated through laboratory experiments and demonstrated accurate estimation of dynamic displacements compared to laser measurements [69].

The application of distributed optical fiber sensors in the monitoring of buildings and bridges has also been demonstrated in the context of digital twins. A case study involving a footbridge at the University of Central Florida used a VR environment to bring the structure and SHM data from the field to the office. The system used UAV photogrammetry and LiDAR methods to capture the bridge, and Finite Element Analysis (FEA) and Operational Modal Analysis (OMA) from vibration data were used for technical assessment. The VR application allowed engineers to make decisions for the footbridge by visualizing the dynamic response of the structure, demonstrating the potential of distributed optical fiber sensors in enabling immersive and collaborative structural health monitoring [32].

In the context of building damage detection, a case study involving a three-span continuous rigid frame bridge structure demonstrated the effectiveness of a hierarchical deep convolutional neural network (CNN) and Gated Recurrent Unit (GRU) framework. The framework, termed HCG, was used to model both spatial and temporal relations for structural damage detection. The system outperformed other existing methods in structural damage detection, showing significant improvements in predictive accuracy and adaptability across multiple SHM scenarios [70].

### 4.8 Use in Smart Cities and Urban Infrastructure

Distributed optical fiber sensors have increasingly become a cornerstone in the development of smart cities and urban infrastructure. As urban areas continue to expand and become more complex, the need for reliable, real-time monitoring of infrastructure has become paramount. These sensors provide a robust solution by enabling continuous and precise monitoring of various parameters such as strain, temperature, and vibration, which are critical for ensuring the safety, resilience, and sustainability of urban environments. The application of distributed optical fiber sensors in smart cities is not just about monitoring; it is about transforming how cities are managed and maintained, making them more efficient, responsive, and adaptable to future challenges.

One of the primary applications of distributed optical fiber sensors in smart cities is the monitoring of urban infrastructure. These sensors are embedded within critical structures such as bridges, buildings, and tunnels, allowing for real-time data collection on their structural health. For instance, in the context of bridges, distributed optical fiber sensors can detect early signs of damage or degradation, such as cracks or corrosion, by continuously monitoring strain and temperature variations [30]. This proactive approach to infrastructure maintenance can significantly reduce the risk of structural failures and ensure the safety of the public. Similarly, in buildings, these sensors can monitor vibrations and deformations, providing valuable insights into the structural integrity of high-rise buildings and other critical structures. By integrating these sensors with advanced data analytics and machine learning algorithms, city planners and engineers can predict potential issues before they escalate into major problems, thereby reducing maintenance costs and extending the lifespan of infrastructure [71].

Moreover, distributed optical fiber sensors play a crucial role in enhancing the resilience of urban infrastructure. In the face of natural disasters such as earthquakes, floods, and extreme weather events, these sensors provide real-time data that can be used to assess the impact on infrastructure and guide emergency response efforts. For example, in earthquake-prone areas, the ability to monitor the structural health of buildings and bridges in real-time can help authorities quickly identify and address potential vulnerabilities, ensuring the safety of residents and minimizing economic losses. Additionally, these sensors can be used to monitor the integrity of underground infrastructure, such as pipelines and tunnels, which are often difficult to access and inspect manually. By detecting leaks, deformations, or other issues early, distributed optical fiber sensors contribute to the overall resilience of urban infrastructure, enabling cities to recover more quickly from disruptive events [53].

In addition to monitoring and resilience, distributed optical fiber sensors also support sustainable development in urban environments. The integration of these sensors with smart city initiatives allows for the efficient use of resources and the reduction of environmental impacts. For instance, in the context of smart grids, distributed optical fiber sensors can monitor the performance of power lines and other electrical infrastructure, helping to identify inefficiencies and reduce energy waste. This not only enhances the reliability of the power supply but also contributes to the overall sustainability of the urban environment. Furthermore, in the realm of transportation, these sensors can be used to monitor the condition of roads, bridges, and other transportation infrastructure, enabling proactive maintenance and reducing the need for costly repairs. By ensuring that infrastructure is maintained in optimal condition, distributed optical fiber sensors help to minimize the environmental footprint of urban development [72].

Another significant application of distributed optical fiber sensors in smart cities is their role in environmental monitoring. These sensors can be deployed to monitor air quality, water quality, and noise levels, providing valuable data that can be used to inform urban planning and policy-making. For example, in the context of air quality monitoring, distributed optical fiber sensors can detect changes in pollutant levels in real-time, allowing city authorities to take immediate action to mitigate the impact of pollution on public health. Similarly, in water management, these sensors can monitor the integrity of water distribution systems, detecting leaks and other issues that could lead to water loss and contamination. By providing accurate and timely data, distributed optical fiber sensors support the development of sustainable and environmentally friendly urban infrastructure [53].

The integration of distributed optical fiber sensors with the Internet of Things (IoT) further enhances their capabilities in smart cities. By connecting these sensors to a network of IoT devices, cities can create a comprehensive monitoring system that provides real-time data on various aspects of urban infrastructure. This connectivity allows for the automation of data collection and analysis, reducing the need for manual intervention and improving the efficiency of urban management. For instance, in the context of traffic monitoring, distributed optical fiber sensors can be used to detect vehicle movements and traffic patterns, providing valuable insights that can be used to optimize traffic flow and reduce congestion. By leveraging the power of IoT, distributed optical fiber sensors contribute to the creation of smarter, more efficient, and more sustainable urban environments [29].

In conclusion, the application of distributed optical fiber sensors in smart cities and urban infrastructure is a transformative approach that enhances the safety, resilience, and sustainability of urban environments. These sensors provide real-time data on the structural health of critical infrastructure, enabling proactive maintenance and reducing the risk of failures. They also support the development of resilient urban systems that can withstand and recover from natural disasters and other disruptions. Furthermore, the integration of these sensors with advanced data analytics, machine learning, and IoT technologies enables cities to make informed decisions and optimize resource usage, contributing to the overall sustainability of urban development. As cities continue to grow and evolve, the role of distributed optical fiber sensors in shaping the future of urban infrastructure will only become more significant [3].

### 4.9 Applications in Heritage and Historical Structures

The application of distributed optical fiber sensors (DOFS) in monitoring heritage and historical structures presents unique challenges and opportunities. Unlike modern infrastructure, historical buildings and monuments often possess complex geometries, diverse materials, and are frequently subject to environmental stresses such as humidity, temperature fluctuations, and mechanical degradation. These factors make the deployment of traditional sensors difficult due to their invasive nature and susceptibility to environmental interference [73]. DOFS, on the other hand, offer non-invasive, continuous, and high-resolution monitoring capabilities, making them particularly suitable for heritage structures. However, the integration of DOFS in such contexts requires tailored solutions that account for the specific characteristics of historical materials and the need for minimal disruption during installation.

One of the primary challenges in using DOFS for heritage structures is the need to preserve the aesthetic and structural integrity of the site. Traditional sensors often require physical attachments or modifications, which may not be acceptable for historical buildings. DOFS, however, can be embedded within the structure or applied as surface-mounted sensors without significantly altering the original appearance. This is especially beneficial for structures with delicate or fragile materials, such as ancient stone or wood. For example, fiber Bragg grating (FBG) sensors can be embedded within masonry or wooden elements to monitor strain and temperature changes, providing early warnings of potential structural issues without damaging the historical fabric [39].

Another challenge is the detection of subtle damage that may not be visible to the naked eye. Historical structures often suffer from micro-cracks, delamination, or internal decay, which can be difficult to identify using conventional inspection methods. DOFS, particularly distributed acoustic sensing (DAS) and Brillouin optical time-domain analysis (BOTDA), are capable of detecting minute changes in strain and temperature along the length of the fiber. These sensors can identify localized damage by analyzing variations in the backscattered light signals [74]. For instance, DAS systems have been used to monitor vibrations in ancient bridges and cathedrals, detecting subtle movements that may indicate structural instability or degradation [75]. The high spatial resolution of DOFS allows for the precise localization of damage, enabling targeted preservation efforts.

The integration of deep learning techniques has further enhanced the capabilities of DOFS in heritage monitoring. Deep learning algorithms, such as convolutional neural networks (CNNs) and long short-term memory (LSTM) networks, have been employed to process and interpret the vast amounts of data generated by DOFS. These algorithms can identify patterns and anomalies in the sensor data that may be indicative of structural damage or environmental stress. For example, a study demonstrated the use of CNNs to classify different types of vibrations recorded by DAS systems, improving the accuracy of damage detection in historical structures [22]. Similarly, LSTM networks have been used to analyze temporal-spatial data matrices from BOTDA systems, enabling the detection of progressive degradation in heritage materials over time [46].

The application of DOFS in heritage structures also involves addressing the issue of environmental variability. Historical buildings are often exposed to fluctuating temperature and humidity levels, which can affect the performance of sensors and the accuracy of measurements. DOFS are generally more robust to environmental variations compared to traditional sensors, but careful calibration and data processing are still required. For example, the use of low-rank constrained multichannel signal denoising techniques has been shown to improve the accuracy of DOFS data by reducing noise and enhancing signal quality [9]. Additionally, the integration of environmental sensors with DOFS can provide contextual data that helps in interpreting the sensor readings, ensuring more reliable and meaningful insights into the structural health of heritage sites.

In addition to technical challenges, the deployment of DOFS in heritage structures also involves considerations related to maintenance and long-term reliability. Historical buildings may not have the infrastructure or resources to support frequent maintenance or sensor recalibration. DOFS, however, are known for their durability and low maintenance requirements, making them a sustainable choice for long-term monitoring. The use of self-supervised learning techniques, such as those employed in the low-rank constrained multichannel signal denoising approach, can further enhance the robustness of DOFS by minimizing the need for manual intervention and improving the adaptability of the system to changing conditions [9].

Moreover, the integration of DOFS with other technologies, such as the Internet of Things (IoT) and cloud computing, offers new possibilities for the remote monitoring and preservation of heritage structures. IoT platforms can facilitate the real-time transmission and analysis of sensor data, enabling proactive maintenance and damage prevention. For example, the TelecomTM system, which uses existing telecommunication fiber-optic cables as virtual sensors, has been successfully applied to monitor traffic and environmental conditions, demonstrating the potential for similar applications in heritage monitoring [28]. Cloud-based data storage and analytics can also support the long-term preservation of historical structures by providing a centralized platform for data management and interpretation.

In conclusion, the application of distributed optical fiber sensors in heritage and historical structures presents a unique set of challenges and opportunities. The non-invasive nature of DOFS, combined with their high sensitivity and spatial resolution, makes them an ideal solution for monitoring the structural health of historical buildings. The integration of deep learning techniques enhances the ability of DOFS to detect and interpret subtle damage, while the use of advanced signal processing methods ensures the reliability and accuracy of the data. Despite the challenges associated with environmental variability, maintenance, and data interpretation, DOFS offer a promising approach for the preservation and monitoring of heritage structures. As research in this field continues to advance, the potential for DOFS to revolutionize the way we protect and maintain historical buildings will only grow.

### 4.10 Future Potential in Large-Scale Infrastructure Monitoring

The future potential of distributed optical fiber sensors (DOFS) in large-scale infrastructure monitoring is a promising and rapidly evolving field. As civil infrastructure becomes more complex and expansive, the need for advanced monitoring systems that can provide real-time, accurate, and comprehensive data on structural health is increasingly critical. DOFS, with their inherent advantages such as high sensitivity, long-range monitoring capabilities, and the ability to provide continuous data, are well-positioned to play a pivotal role in the future of large-scale infrastructure monitoring. The advancements in sensor networks, data analytics, and integration with emerging technologies are expected to further enhance the capabilities of DOFS, making them a cornerstone of intelligent and resilient civil engineering systems.

One of the key areas of future development is the advancement of sensor networks. Traditional sensor networks often face limitations in terms of scalability, reliability, and data interpretation. However, DOFS can overcome these challenges by providing a single, continuous sensing medium that can cover vast areas with minimal infrastructure. The integration of DOFS with wireless sensor networks (WSNs) and the Internet of Things (IoT) is expected to enhance the scalability and flexibility of monitoring systems. For instance, the use of DOFS in conjunction with WSNs can enable the deployment of large-scale sensor networks that can monitor entire infrastructure systems in real-time [53]. This integration not only improves data collection but also enhances the ability to analyze and interpret data from multiple sources, leading to more accurate and comprehensive monitoring.

Another significant area of future development is the advancement of data analytics techniques. The vast amount of data generated by DOFS requires sophisticated analytics to extract meaningful insights. Machine learning and artificial intelligence (AI) are expected to play a crucial role in this regard. By leveraging deep learning algorithms, it is possible to automate the process of data interpretation and anomaly detection, leading to faster and more accurate decision-making. For example, the integration of DOFS with deep learning models can enable the real-time monitoring of structural health by detecting subtle changes in strain, temperature, and vibration patterns [24]. These techniques can also help in predicting potential failures and enabling proactive maintenance, thereby enhancing the safety and longevity of infrastructure.

The role of DOFS in creating intelligent and resilient civil engineering systems is another critical area of future potential. As cities and infrastructure become more interconnected, the need for systems that can adapt to changing conditions and respond to emerging challenges is paramount. DOFS can contribute to the development of smart cities by providing real-time data on the condition of infrastructure, enabling the optimization of resource allocation and the enhancement of urban resilience. For example, the integration of DOFS with digital twin systems can enable the creation of virtual replicas of infrastructure that can be used for simulation, prediction, and decision-making [31]. This approach can significantly enhance the ability to monitor and manage infrastructure, leading to more efficient and sustainable urban development.

In addition to these advancements, the future potential of DOFS in large-scale infrastructure monitoring also includes the development of more robust and cost-effective sensor technologies. While DOFS have already demonstrated their effectiveness in various applications, there is a need for further innovations to enhance their performance and reduce costs. The development of more sensitive and durable sensors, as well as the integration of multiple sensing modalities, can further expand the capabilities of DOFS. For instance, the use of DOFS in combination with other sensor technologies such as piezoelectric sensors and strain gauges can provide a more comprehensive understanding of structural health [76]. These hybrid systems can offer enhanced accuracy and reliability, making them suitable for a wide range of applications.

Moreover, the future of DOFS in large-scale infrastructure monitoring is closely tied to the advancements in communication technologies. The integration of DOFS with 6G and other next-generation communication technologies can enable high-speed, low-latency, and reliable data transmission, which is essential for real-time monitoring. This integration can also facilitate the development of more sophisticated monitoring systems that can handle large volumes of data and provide real-time insights [77]. As these technologies continue to evolve, the potential for DOFS to revolutionize infrastructure monitoring will only grow.

In conclusion, the future potential of distributed optical fiber sensors in large-scale infrastructure monitoring is vast and multifaceted. The advancements in sensor networks, data analytics, and integration with emerging technologies are expected to significantly enhance the capabilities of DOFS. These developments will not only improve the accuracy and efficiency of infrastructure monitoring but also contribute to the creation of intelligent and resilient civil engineering systems. As research and innovation continue to progress, DOFS are poised to play a central role in the future of infrastructure monitoring, ensuring the safety, durability, and sustainability of civil infrastructure.

## 5 Signal Processing and Data Analysis Techniques

### 5.1 Noise Reduction Techniques

Noise reduction is a critical step in the signal processing pipeline for distributed optical fiber sensors, as the raw data collected from these systems often contains various types of noise that can obscure the underlying structural information. The presence of noise can significantly affect the accuracy of damage detection, strain monitoring, and other structural health monitoring (SHM) tasks. Therefore, effective noise reduction techniques are essential to ensure the reliability and usefulness of the data obtained from distributed optical fiber sensors.

Traditional signal processing methods have long been used to reduce noise in sensor data. These methods include filtering techniques such as low-pass, high-pass, and band-pass filters, which can be used to remove unwanted frequency components from the data. For example, low-pass filters are commonly used to eliminate high-frequency noise while preserving the lower frequency components that are typically associated with structural responses. Similarly, high-pass filters can be employed to remove low-frequency drift or DC offsets that may be present in the data. Band-pass filters are particularly useful in scenarios where the signal of interest lies within a specific frequency range, and noise exists outside of this range. These traditional methods are computationally efficient and can be implemented in real-time systems, making them suitable for applications where processing speed is a concern [18].

Another widely used technique for noise reduction is wavelet transforms. Unlike traditional Fourier-based methods, wavelet transforms provide a time-frequency representation of the signal, allowing for the separation of noise from the actual structural response. This makes wavelet transforms particularly effective in dealing with non-stationary signals, which are common in SHM applications. The process involves decomposing the signal into different frequency bands using wavelets, followed by thresholding to remove or reduce the noise components. The thresholding can be performed using hard or soft thresholding techniques, depending on the desired level of noise reduction and the characteristics of the signal. Wavelet-based methods have been successfully applied in various SHM contexts, including vibration analysis and damage detection in bridges and buildings [18].

In addition to traditional signal processing methods, machine learning-based approaches have gained increasing attention in recent years for noise reduction in distributed optical fiber sensor data. These methods leverage the power of deep learning and other advanced algorithms to automatically learn the characteristics of the noise and the desired signal, enabling more effective noise removal. One such technique is the DAS-N2N (Distributed Acoustic Sensing - Noise to Noise) method, which is specifically designed for denoising without the need for clean data. This approach utilizes a deep neural network to map noisy sensor data to clean data, effectively learning the noise patterns and eliminating them from the signal. The DAS-N2N method has been shown to outperform traditional filtering and wavelet-based techniques in several real-world applications, particularly in scenarios where the noise characteristics are complex and non-stationary [18].

Another promising machine learning-based approach for noise reduction is the use of autoencoders. Autoencoders are neural networks that are trained to reconstruct their input data, effectively learning a compressed representation of the data that captures the essential features while discarding the noise. In the context of distributed optical fiber sensors, autoencoders can be trained on clean data to learn the underlying patterns, and then used to denoise new data by reconstructing the input. This approach has been successfully applied in several SHM studies, including the detection of structural damage in bridges and buildings [20].

Deep learning techniques, such as convolutional neural networks (CNNs) and recurrent neural networks (RNNs), have also been explored for noise reduction in distributed optical fiber sensor data. These models can capture the spatial and temporal dependencies in the data, making them particularly effective in dealing with complex noise patterns. For instance, CNNs can be used to extract local features from the sensor data, while RNNs can model the temporal dynamics of the signal. By combining these techniques, it is possible to develop robust noise reduction algorithms that can handle a wide range of noise sources and signal characteristics [18].

Furthermore, the integration of physics-based models with machine learning techniques has shown great potential in improving noise reduction performance. By incorporating the physical principles governing the behavior of the structure, these hybrid models can more accurately distinguish between noise and actual structural responses. For example, physics-informed neural networks (PINNs) can be trained to not only denoise the sensor data but also to ensure that the reconstructed signal adheres to the underlying physical laws. This approach has been shown to be particularly effective in applications where the signal of interest is influenced by complex physical phenomena, such as those encountered in structural dynamics and vibration analysis [78].

In addition to the above-mentioned techniques, there are several other advanced methods that can be used for noise reduction in distributed optical fiber sensor data. These include sparse representation techniques, which assume that the signal can be represented as a sparse combination of basis functions, and compressive sensing, which allows for the reconstruction of the signal from a small number of measurements. These techniques are particularly useful in scenarios where the number of sensors is limited or where the data acquisition process is constrained. By leveraging the sparsity of the signal, these methods can effectively reduce noise while preserving the essential features of the data [45].

In summary, noise reduction is a critical component of the signal processing pipeline for distributed optical fiber sensors. Traditional signal processing methods such as filtering and wavelet transforms have been widely used and are effective in many applications. However, the increasing complexity of the data and the need for more accurate and robust noise reduction techniques have led to the development of advanced machine learning-based approaches. Techniques such as autoencoders, deep neural networks, and physics-informed models have shown great promise in improving the performance of noise reduction algorithms. By combining these methods with traditional signal processing techniques, it is possible to develop more effective noise reduction strategies that can enhance the accuracy and reliability of structural health monitoring systems. As the field of SHM continues to evolve, the development of novel noise reduction techniques will remain an important area of research, driven by the need for more accurate and efficient monitoring of civil infrastructure.

### 5.2 Feature Extraction Methods

Feature extraction is a crucial step in the analysis of data from distributed optical fiber sensors, as it involves identifying and isolating the most relevant information from the raw data for further processing and interpretation. These sensors, such as Fiber Bragg Grating (FBG), Distributed Acoustic Sensing (DAS), and Brillouin Optical Time-Domain Analysis (BOTDA), generate complex temporal-spatial data matrices that require advanced feature extraction techniques to extract meaningful insights. This subsection explores various feature extraction methods, including time-domain, frequency-domain, and spatial-domain features, and highlights the use of convolutional neural networks (CNNs) and long short-term memory (LSTM) networks for extracting meaningful features from temporal-spatial data matrices.

In the time-domain feature extraction approach, the raw data is analyzed for specific characteristics such as amplitude, duration, and temporal patterns. This method is particularly effective in identifying transient events, such as vibrations or strain changes, which are critical for structural health monitoring (SHM). For example, in DAS applications, time-domain features can be extracted by analyzing the amplitude and timing of the backscattered signals, which can indicate the presence of disturbances along the fiber optic cable [6]. Time-domain features are also useful for detecting anomalies in real-time monitoring systems, where quick response is essential. However, this method can be limited in capturing complex patterns that may require more advanced signal processing techniques.

Frequency-domain feature extraction involves transforming the time-domain data into the frequency domain using techniques such as Fast Fourier Transform (FFT). This method is effective in identifying periodic patterns and frequency components that may be indicative of structural damage or degradation. For instance, in the case of FBG sensors, frequency-domain features can be extracted by analyzing the reflected wavelength of light, which is sensitive to changes in strain and temperature [7]. This approach allows for the identification of subtle changes in the structural behavior that may not be apparent in the time-domain analysis. However, the transformation process can be computationally intensive, and the interpretation of frequency-domain features may require domain-specific knowledge.

Spatial-domain feature extraction focuses on the spatial distribution of the sensor data across the monitored structure. This method is particularly relevant for distributed optical fiber sensors, as they provide continuous spatial coverage along the fiber optic cable. Spatial-domain features can be extracted by analyzing the spatial distribution of strain, temperature, or vibration data, which can provide insights into the overall structural integrity. For example, in the case of DAS applications, spatial-domain features can be extracted by analyzing the spatial distribution of the backscattered signals, which can indicate the location and magnitude of disturbances along the fiber [6]. This method is effective in identifying localized damage or anomalies, but it can be challenging to interpret the spatial features in the context of the entire structure.

In addition to these traditional feature extraction methods, modern machine learning techniques, such as convolutional neural networks (CNNs) and long short-term memory (LSTMs) networks, have shown great promise in extracting meaningful features from temporal-spatial data matrices. CNNs are particularly effective in capturing spatial features by applying convolutional filters to the data, which can detect patterns and structures that are not easily identifiable through traditional methods [46]. For instance, in the case of Phase-OTDR systems, CNNs can be used to extract spatial features from the differential amplitude and phase information, which can be used for event recognition and classification [46]. This approach has been shown to achieve high classification accuracy, with some studies reporting 100% accuracy in identifying different types of vibrations [46].

LSTMs, on the other hand, are well-suited for analyzing time-series data and capturing long-term temporal dependencies. They are particularly effective in extracting temporal features from the data, which can be used for event detection and anomaly recognition. For example, in the context of DAS applications, LSTMs can be used to analyze the temporal patterns of the backscattered signals, which can provide insights into the nature and location of disturbances along the fiber [46]. This approach has been shown to be effective in detecting and classifying different types of events, such as vehicle movements or structural vibrations [8]. LSTMs are also useful in capturing the temporal dynamics of the data, which can be crucial for real-time monitoring systems that require quick response times [8].

The integration of CNNs and LSTMs in feature extraction from temporal-spatial data matrices has been shown to significantly improve the accuracy and robustness of structural health monitoring systems. For instance, in the case of distributed fiber optic vibration event recognition, a CNN-based approach has been used to extract spatial features from the data, while an LSTM network has been used to capture the temporal dynamics of the signals [24]. This combination has been shown to achieve high accuracy in real-time event recognition, with minimal computational overhead [24]. Similarly, in the case of modal analysis, a combination of CNNs and LSTMs has been used to extract both spatial and temporal features from the data, which has been shown to improve the accuracy of damage detection and localization [45].

Moreover, the use of feature extraction techniques in conjunction with deep learning models has been shown to enhance the interpretability and explainability of the results. For example, in the case of damage detection in bridges, a combination of CNNs and LSTMs has been used to extract meaningful features from the data, which has been shown to improve the accuracy of damage detection and localization [30]. This approach has also been shown to provide insights into the underlying structural behavior, which can be useful for maintenance and repair decisions.

In conclusion, feature extraction is a critical step in the analysis of data from distributed optical fiber sensors. The use of time-domain, frequency-domain, and spatial-domain features, combined with advanced machine learning techniques such as CNNs and LSTMs, has shown great promise in extracting meaningful insights from the data. These methods have been successfully applied in various applications, including vibration detection, damage localization, and real-time monitoring. The integration of these techniques has the potential to significantly enhance the accuracy, robustness, and efficiency of structural health monitoring systems, making them more effective in ensuring the safety and durability of civil infrastructure.

### 5.3 Machine Learning and Artificial Intelligence Integration

The integration of machine learning (ML) and artificial intelligence (AI) techniques into distributed optical fiber sensing has revolutionized the way data is interpreted and decisions are made in structural health monitoring (SHM) systems. These advanced computational methods enable the extraction of meaningful information from vast and complex datasets, enhancing the accuracy, efficiency, and reliability of SHM systems. Deep learning models, such as pre-trained convolutional neural networks (CNNs) and long short-term memory (LSTMs), have emerged as powerful tools for event recognition and classification, while federated learning (FL) has introduced new paradigms for privacy-preserving data processing. These techniques are not only improving the performance of distributed optical fiber sensing systems but also addressing critical challenges such as data interpretation, noise reduction, and real-time decision-making.

Deep learning models, particularly CNNs and LSTMs, have been extensively applied in the analysis of data from distributed optical fiber sensors. CNNs are well-suited for extracting spatial features from temporal-spatial data matrices, making them ideal for tasks such as event recognition and classification in distributed acoustic sensing (DAS) systems. For instance, a study demonstrated the effectiveness of combining pre-trained CNNs with LSTMs for classifying vibrations applied to an optical fiber by a piezo transducer. The study used a multi-input multi-output, two-stage feature extraction methodology, where the first stage employed a pre-trained CNN without dense layers to extract differential amplitude and phase information, while the second stage used LSTMs to further analyze the features extracted by the CNN [46]. The results showed that the proposed framework achieved 100% classification accuracy in 50 trainings, highlighting the potential of deep learning in enhancing the performance of DAS systems.

In addition to CNNs, LSTMs have been widely used for processing sequential data in distributed optical fiber sensing applications. LSTMs are particularly effective in capturing long-term dependencies in time-series data, making them suitable for tasks such as vibration analysis and damage detection in civil infrastructure. A study demonstrated the application of LSTMs in classifying vehicle types and estimating vehicle occupancy using DAS data collected under controlled conditions. The proposed deep learning method achieved 92% vehicle classification accuracy and 92-97% in occupancy detection, showcasing the versatility of LSTMs in handling complex data from distributed optical fiber sensors [8].

Federated learning (FL) has also gained significant attention in the context of distributed optical fiber sensing, particularly for privacy-preserving data processing. FL allows multiple devices or nodes to collaboratively train a machine learning model without sharing raw data, thereby protecting sensitive information. In the context of distributed optical fiber sensing, FL can be used to train models for anomaly detection, fault diagnosis, and damage localization while ensuring data privacy. For example, a study on federated learning for distributed spectrum sensing in next-generation (NextG) communication networks demonstrated the feasibility of using FL to collectively train a deep neural network (DNN) for signal identification. The study showed that FL could achieve high signal classification accuracy while preserving the privacy of signals collected at different locations [79].

Moreover, the integration of ML and AI techniques with distributed optical fiber sensing has enabled real-time processing and decision-making. Real-time processing is crucial for applications such as structural health monitoring, where timely detection of anomalies and faults is essential for ensuring safety and reliability. The use of edge computing and lightweight models has further enhanced the efficiency of real-time processing in distributed optical fiber sensing systems. For instance, a study on the real-time FPGA implementation of CNN-based distributed fiber optic vibration event recognition methods demonstrated the effectiveness of using field-programmable gate arrays (FPGAs) for low-power and efficient inference. The study showed that a fully on-chip, pipelined inference accelerator for CNN-based DVS algorithms could achieve real-time processing with minimal power consumption and system requirements [24].

In addition to real-time processing, ML and AI techniques have also improved the interpretability and explainability of data from distributed optical fiber sensors. As deep learning models become more complex, there is a growing need for methods that can provide insights into the decision-making process. Techniques such as visualization, feature manipulation, and model interpretability have been used to understand how deep learning models make predictions. For example, a study on the interpretation of deep learning models in distributed optical fiber sensing emphasized the importance of data interpretation and explainability, highlighting methods such as visualization techniques and feature manipulation to improve transparency in signal analysis [22].

The integration of ML and AI techniques in distributed optical fiber sensing has also addressed challenges related to data interpretation and noise reduction. Noise reduction is a critical step in processing data from distributed optical fiber sensors, as noise can significantly affect the accuracy of event recognition and classification. Deep learning techniques have been used to develop advanced noise reduction methods, such as denoising convolutional autoencoders (DCAEs) and bidirectional LSTMs. A study on the use of DCAEs and BiLSTMs for noise reduction in OTDR traces demonstrated the effectiveness of these techniques in improving the accuracy of fault detection and localization [80].

Overall, the integration of machine learning and artificial intelligence techniques in distributed optical fiber sensing has significantly enhanced the capabilities of structural health monitoring systems. By leveraging deep learning models such as CNNs and LSTMs, as well as federated learning for privacy-preserving data processing, these techniques have improved the accuracy, efficiency, and reliability of data interpretation and decision-making. As the field continues to evolve, further research into the application of ML and AI in distributed optical fiber sensing will be essential for addressing emerging challenges and unlocking new opportunities in structural health monitoring.

### 5.4 Data Reconstruction and Compressive Sensing

Data reconstruction and compressive sensing (CS) play a crucial role in the processing of data obtained from distributed optical fiber sensors, especially in scenarios where the signal is sparse or the data acquisition process is constrained. Compressive sensing is a revolutionary technique that enables the reconstruction of signals from a small number of measurements, far below the Nyquist rate, by exploiting the sparsity of the signal in a certain domain. This approach is particularly useful for distributed optical fiber sensors, where the high-dimensional data streams can be challenging to process and store efficiently [45].

The CS framework is based on the principle that many real-world signals are sparse or can be represented sparsely in some transform domain. This allows for the recovery of the original signal using a limited number of measurements, provided that the measurement matrix satisfies certain conditions, such as the restricted isometry property (RIP). In the context of distributed optical fiber sensors, CS can significantly reduce the amount of data that needs to be transmitted and stored, thus improving the efficiency of the monitoring system [45].

The integration of machine learning-based approaches has further enhanced the capabilities of data reconstruction in distributed optical fiber sensors. These methods leverage the power of neural networks to model the complex relationships between the sparse measurements and the original signal. For instance, deep learning techniques, such as convolutional neural networks (CNNs) and recurrent neural networks (RNNs), have been used to enhance the accuracy of signal recovery by learning the underlying patterns in the data. By training these models on large datasets, it is possible to achieve high-quality reconstructions even from a small number of measurements [12].

One of the key advantages of using neural networks for compressive sensing-based data reconstruction is their ability to handle non-linear and non-Gaussian noise, which is common in real-world sensor data. Traditional CS methods often assume that the noise is Gaussian and additive, which may not always be the case in practical applications. Neural networks, on the other hand, can learn to adapt to the specific characteristics of the noise in the data, leading to more robust and accurate reconstructions. This has been demonstrated in several studies where deep learning models outperformed traditional CS algorithms in terms of both reconstruction accuracy and computational efficiency [12].

In addition to neural networks, other machine learning techniques, such as support vector machines (SVMs) and random forests, have also been explored for data reconstruction in distributed optical fiber sensors. These methods are particularly useful for handling high-dimensional data and can be effective in scenarios where the signal is not strictly sparse. However, they often require careful feature selection and tuning of hyperparameters to achieve optimal performance. This highlights the importance of domain knowledge and expertise in the design and implementation of data reconstruction algorithms [12].

The integration of compressive sensing with machine learning techniques has led to the development of hybrid approaches that combine the strengths of both methodologies. For example, some studies have proposed using CS to reduce the dimensionality of the data before applying machine learning algorithms for further processing. This two-step approach can significantly improve the efficiency of the data reconstruction process while maintaining a high level of accuracy. The effectiveness of such hybrid methods has been validated through various experiments, demonstrating their potential for practical applications in structural health monitoring [12].

Another important aspect of data reconstruction in distributed optical fiber sensors is the choice of the measurement matrix. The measurement matrix determines how the signal is projected onto the lower-dimensional space and plays a critical role in the success of the CS algorithm. Commonly used measurement matrices include random Gaussian matrices, random Bernoulli matrices, and partial Fourier matrices. The choice of the measurement matrix depends on the specific characteristics of the signal and the constraints of the application. For instance, in applications where the signal is sparse in the frequency domain, a partial Fourier matrix may be more suitable than a random Gaussian matrix [45].

In addition to the measurement matrix, the reconstruction algorithm itself is a crucial component of the CS framework. Popular reconstruction algorithms include basis pursuit (BP), orthogonal matching pursuit (OMP), and iterative thresholding methods. These algorithms differ in terms of their computational complexity, convergence speed, and robustness to noise. The selection of the appropriate reconstruction algorithm depends on the specific requirements of the application, such as the desired level of accuracy, the available computational resources, and the characteristics of the noise in the data. For example, in applications where real-time processing is required, a fast and efficient algorithm such as OMP may be preferred over a more accurate but computationally intensive algorithm like BP [45].

The use of compressive sensing in distributed optical fiber sensors also raises important questions about the trade-offs between the number of measurements, the reconstruction accuracy, and the computational complexity.
